			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Partition of India</h1>
									<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
									<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
								<div id="contentSub"></div>
												<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">

<div>The British Indian Empire, from the 1909 edition of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Imperial_Gazetteer_of_India" title="The Imperial Gazetteer of India">The Imperial Gazetteer of India</a></i>. <tr>
<th scope="row">Deaths</th>
<td>200,000 to 2,000,000<br />
<sup id="cite_ref-D.27Costa_2011_53_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-D.27Costa_2011_53-1">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Butalia_2000_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Butalia_2000-3">[3]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The <b>Partition of India</b> was the 1947 <a href="/wiki/Partition_(politics)" title="Partition (politics)">partitioning</a> of the <a href="/wiki/British_Indian_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="British Indian Empire">British Indian Empire</a> into India and Pakistan.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> It led to the creation of the <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_state" title="Sovereign state">sovereign states</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Dominion_of_Pakistan" title="Dominion of Pakistan">Dominion of Pakistan</a> (which later split into <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>) and the <a href="/wiki/Union_of_India" class="mw-redirect" title="Union of India">Union of India</a> (later <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Republic_of_India" title="History of the Republic of India">Republic of India</a>) on 14–15 August 1947. "Partition" here refers not only to the <a href="/wiki/Partition_of_Bengal_(1947)" title="Partition of Bengal (1947)">division</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Bengal" title="Bengal">Bengal</a> province of <a href="/wiki/British_India" class="mw-redirect" title="British India">British India</a> into <a href="/wiki/East_Pakistan" title="East Pakistan">East Pakistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/West_Bengal" title="West Bengal">West Bengal</a> (India), and the similar partition of the <a href="/wiki/Punjab_Province_(British_India)" title="Punjab Province (British India)">Punjab Province</a> into <a href="/wiki/Punjab,_Pakistan" title="Punjab, Pakistan">West Punjab</a> (<a href="/wiki/West_Pakistan" title="West Pakistan">West Pakistan</a>) and <a href="/wiki/East_Punjab" title="East Punjab">East Punjab</a> (now <a href="/wiki/Punjab,_India" title="Punjab, India">Punjab</a>), but also to the respective divisions of other assets, including the <a href="/wiki/British_Indian_Army" title="British Indian Army">British Indian Army</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Civil_Service" class="mw-redirect" title="Indian Civil Service">Indian Civil Service</a> and other administrative services, the <a href="/wiki/History_of_rail_transport_in_India" title="History of rail transport in India">railways</a>, and the central treasury.</p>
<p>In the riots which preceded the partition in the Punjab Province, it is believed that between 200,000 and 2,000,000<sup id="cite_ref-D.27Costa_2011_53_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-D.27Costa_2011_53-1">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Butalia_2000_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Butalia_2000-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> people were killed in the retributive genocide between the religions.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/UNHCR" class="mw-redirect" title="UNHCR">UNHCR</a> estimates 14 million Hindus, Sikhs and Muslims were displaced during the partition; it was the largest <a href="/wiki/Mass_migration" title="Mass migration">mass migration</a> in human history.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>The term <i>partition of India</i> does not cover the later <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh_Liberation_War" title="Bangladesh Liberation War">secession of Bangladesh from Pakistan</a> in 1971, nor the earlier separation of <a href="/wiki/Burma" class="mw-redirect" title="Burma">Burma</a> (now known as <a href="/wiki/Myanmar" title="Myanmar">Myanmar</a>) from the administration of British India, nor the separation of <a href="/wiki/Ceylon" class="mw-redirect" title="Ceylon">Ceylon</a> (now <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a>). The coastal area of Ceylon was part of the <a href="/wiki/Madras_Presidency" title="Madras Presidency">Madras Presidency</a> of British India from 1795 until 1798, when it became a separate <a href="/wiki/Crown_Colony" class="mw-redirect" title="Crown Colony">Crown Colony</a> of the Empire. Burma, gradually annexed by the British during 1826–86 and governed as a part of the British Indian administration until 1937, was directly administered thereafter.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Burma was granted independence on 4 January 1948 and Ceylon on 4 February 1948. (See <a href="/wiki/History_of_Sri_Lanka" title="History of Sri Lanka">History of Sri Lanka</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Burma" class="mw-redirect" title="History of Burma">History of Burma</a>.)</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Bhutan" title="Bhutan">Bhutan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Nepal" title="Kingdom of Nepal">Nepal</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Maldives" title="Maldives">Maldives</a>, the remaining present-day countries of South Asia, were unaffected by the partition. The first two, Bhutan and Nepal, although earlier being regarded as de facto <a href="/wiki/Princely_states" class="mw-redirect" title="Princely states">princely states</a>, later signed treaties with the British designating them as <i>independent states</i> before partition, and therefore their borders were unaffected by the partition of India.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> The Maldives, which had become a <a href="/wiki/Protectorate" title="Protectorate">protectorate</a> of the <a href="/wiki/British_crown" class="mw-redirect" title="British crown">British crown</a> in 1887 and gained its independence in 1965, was also unaffected by the partition.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Partition_of_India&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Partition_of_Bengal_.281905.29">Partition of Bengal (1905)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Partition_of_India&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Partition of Bengal (1905)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Partition_of_Bengal_(1905)" title="Partition of Bengal (1905)">Partition of Bengal (1905)</a></div>
<table style="background:transparent;margin-top:0.5em;margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto">

<p>In 1905, the viceroy, <a href="/wiki/Lord_Curzon" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Curzon">Lord Curzon</a>, in his second term, divided the largest administrative subdivision in British India, the <a href="/wiki/Bengal_Presidency" title="Bengal Presidency">Bengal Presidency</a>, into the Muslim-majority province of <i><a href="/wiki/East_Bengal" title="East Bengal">East Bengal and Assam</a></i> and the Hindu-majority <i>province</i> of <i>Bengal</i> (present-day Indian states of <a href="/wiki/West_Bengal" title="West Bengal">West Bengal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bihar" title="Bihar">Bihār</a>, Jharkhand and <a href="/wiki/Odisha" title="Odisha">Odisha</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-spear176_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-spear176-13">[13]</a></sup> Curzon's act, the <a href="/wiki/Partition_of_Bengal_(1905)" title="Partition of Bengal (1905)">Partition of Bengal</a>—which some considered administratively felicitous<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (May 2016)">by whom?</span></a></i>]</sup>, and, which had been contemplated by various colonial administrations since the time of <a href="/wiki/Lord_William_Bentinck" title="Lord William Bentinck">Lord William Bentinck</a>, but never acted upon—was to transform nationalist politics as nothing else before it.<sup id="cite_ref-spear176_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-spear176-13">[13]</a></sup> The Hindu elite of Bengal, among them many who owned land in East Bengal that was leased out to Muslim peasants, protested fervidly. The large Bengali Hindu middle-class (the <i><a href="/wiki/Bhadralok" title="Bhadralok">Bhadralok</a></i>), upset at the prospect of Bengalis being outnumbered in the new Bengal province by Biharis and Oriyas, felt that Curzon's act was punishment for their political assertiveness.<sup id="cite_ref-spear176_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-spear176-13">[13]</a></sup> The pervasive protests against Curzon's decision took the form predominantly of the <i><a href="/wiki/Swadeshi" class="mw-redirect" title="Swadeshi">Swadeshi</a></i> ("buy Indian") campaign led by two-time Congress president, <a href="/wiki/Surendranath_Banerjee" title="Surendranath Banerjee">Surendranath Banerjee</a>, and involved boycott of British goods. Sporadically—but flagrantly—the protesters also took to political violence that involved attacks on civilians.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> The violence, however, was not effective, as most planned attacks were either preempted by the British or failed.<sup id="cite_ref-bandyo260_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bandyo260-15">[15]</a></sup> The rallying cry for both types of protest was the slogan <i><a href="/wiki/Bande_Mataram" class="mw-redirect" title="Bande Mataram">Bande Mataram</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Bengali_language" title="Bengali language">Bengali</a>, lit: "Hail to the Mother"), the title of a song by <a href="/wiki/Bankim_Chandra_Chatterjee" class="mw-redirect" title="Bankim Chandra Chatterjee">Bankim Chandra Chatterjee</a>, which invoked a mother goddess, who stood variously for Bengal, India, and the Hindu goddess <a href="/wiki/Kali" title="Kali">Kali</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ludden193_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ludden193-16">[16]</a></sup> The unrest spread from Calcutta to the surrounding regions of Bengal when Calcutta's English-educated students returned home to their villages and towns.<sup id="cite_ref-ludden199_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ludden199-17">[17]</a></sup> The religious stirrings of the slogan and the political outrage over the partition were combined as young men, in groups such as <a href="/wiki/Jugantar" title="Jugantar">Jugantar</a>, took to bombing public buildings, staging armed robberies,<sup id="cite_ref-bandyo260_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bandyo260-15">[15]</a></sup> and assassinating British officials.<sup id="cite_ref-ludden193_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ludden193-16">[16]</a></sup> Since Calcutta was the imperial capital, both the outrage and the slogan soon became nationally known.<sup id="cite_ref-ludden193_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ludden193-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>The overwhelming, but predominantly Hindu, protest against the partition of Bengal and the fear, in its wake, of reforms favouring the Hindu majority, now led the Muslim elite in India, in 1906, to meet with the new viceroy, <a href="/wiki/Lord_Minto" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Minto">Lord Minto</a>, and to ask for separate electorates for Muslims. In conjunction, they demanded proportional legislative representation reflecting both their status as former rulers and their record of cooperating with the British. This led, in December 1906, to the founding of the <a href="/wiki/All-India_Muslim_League" title="All-India Muslim League">All-India Muslim League</a> in <a href="/wiki/Dhaka" title="Dhaka">Dacca</a>. Although Curzon, by now, had resigned his position over a dispute with his military chief <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Kitchener,_1st_Earl_Kitchener" title="Herbert Kitchener, 1st Earl Kitchener">Lord Kitchener</a> and returned to England, the League was in favour of his partition plan. The Muslim elite's position, which was reflected in the League's position, had crystallized gradually over the previous three decades, beginning with the 1871 Census of British India, which had first estimated the populations in regions of Muslim majority.<sup id="cite_ref-ludden200_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ludden200-18">[18]</a></sup> (For his part, Curzon's desire to court the Muslims of East Bengal had arisen from British anxieties ever since the 1871 census—and in light of the history of Muslims fighting them in the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Mutiny_of_1857" class="mw-redirect" title="Indian Mutiny of 1857">1857 Mutiny</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Second_Anglo-Afghan_War" title="Second Anglo-Afghan War">Second Anglo-Afghan War</a>—about Indian Muslims rebelling against the Crown.<sup id="cite_ref-ludden200_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ludden200-18">[18]</a></sup>) In the three decades since that census, Muslim leaders across northern India, had intermittently experienced public animosity from some of the new Hindu political and social groups.<sup id="cite_ref-ludden200_18-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ludden200-18">[18]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Arya_Samaj" title="Arya Samaj">Arya Samaj</a>, for example, had not only supported Cow Protection Societies in their agitation,<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> but also—distraught at the 1871 Census's Muslim numbers—organized "reconversion" events for the purpose of welcoming Muslims back to the Hindu fold.<sup id="cite_ref-ludden200_18-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ludden200-18">[18]</a></sup> In UP, Muslims became anxious when, in the late 19th century, political representation increased, giving more power to Hindus, and Hindus were politically mobilized in the Hindi-Urdu controversy and the anti-cow-killing riots of 1893.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200920_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200920-20">[20]</a></sup> In 1905, when <a href="/wiki/Lokmanya_Tilak" class="mw-redirect" title="Lokmanya Tilak">Tilak</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lala_Lajpat_Rai" title="Lala Lajpat Rai">Lajpat Rai</a> attempted to rise to leadership positions in the Congress, and the Congress itself rallied around symbolism of Kali, Muslim fears increased.<sup id="cite_ref-ludden200_18-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ludden200-18">[18]</a></sup> It was not lost on many Muslims, for example, that the rallying cry, "Bande Mataram," had first appeared in the novel <i><a href="/wiki/Anand_Math" title="Anand Math">Anand Math</a></i> in which Hindus had battled their Muslim oppressors.<sup id="cite_ref-ludden201_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ludden201-21">[21]</a></sup> Lastly, the Muslim elite, and among it <a href="/wiki/Dhaka_Nawab_Family" class="mw-redirect" title="Dhaka Nawab Family">Dacca Nawab</a>, <a href="/wiki/Khwaja_Salimullah" title="Khwaja Salimullah">Khwaja Salimullah</a>, who hosted the League's first meeting in his mansion in <a href="/wiki/Shahbag" title="Shahbag">Shahbag</a>, was aware that a new province with a Muslim majority would directly benefit Muslims aspiring to political power.<sup id="cite_ref-ludden201_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ludden201-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_I.2C_Lucknow_Pact:_1914.E2.80.931918">World War I, Lucknow Pact: 1914–1918</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Partition_of_India&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: World War I, Lucknow Pact: 1914–1918">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<p><a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> would prove to be a watershed in the imperial relationship between Britain and India. 1.4 million Indian and British soldiers of the <a href="/wiki/British_Indian_Army" title="British Indian Army">British Indian Army</a> would take part in the war and their participation would have a wider cultural fallout: news of Indian soldiers fighting and dying with British soldiers, as well as soldiers from <a href="/wiki/Dominion" title="Dominion">dominions</a> like Canada and Australia, would travel to distant corners of the world both in newsprint and by the new medium of the radio.<sup id="cite_ref-brown-p197-198_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brown-p197-198-22">[22]</a></sup> India's international profile would thereby rise and would continue to rise during the 1920s.<sup id="cite_ref-brown-p197-198_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brown-p197-198-22">[22]</a></sup> It was to lead, among other things, to India, under its own name, becoming a <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations_members#1920:_founder_members" class="mw-redirect" title="League of Nations members">founding member</a> of the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a> in 1920 and participating, under the name, "Les Indes Anglaises" (British India), in the <a href="/wiki/1920_Summer_Olympics" title="1920 Summer Olympics">1920 Summer Olympics</a> in Antwerp.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Back in India, especially among the leaders of the <a href="/wiki/Indian_National_Congress" title="Indian National Congress">Indian National Congress</a>, it would lead to calls for greater self-government for Indians.<sup id="cite_ref-brown-p197-198_22-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brown-p197-198-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>The 1916 Lucknow Session of the Congress was also the venue of an unanticipated mutual effort by the Congress and the Muslim League, the occasion for which was provided by the wartime partnership between Germany and Turkey. Since the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Caliphate" title="Ottoman Caliphate">Turkish Sultan</a>, or <i>Khalifah</i>, had also sporadically claimed guardianship of the Islamic holy sites of <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a>, <a href="/wiki/Medina" title="Medina">Medina</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a>, and since the British and their allies were now in conflict with Turkey, doubts began to increase among some Indian Muslims about the "religious neutrality" of the British, doubts that had already surfaced as a result of the <a href="/wiki/Partition_of_Bengal_(1905)" title="Partition of Bengal (1905)">reunification of Bengal</a> in 1911, a decision that was seen as ill-disposed to Muslims.<sup id="cite_ref-brown-p200-201_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brown-p200-201-24">[24]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Lucknow_Pact" title="Lucknow Pact">Lucknow Pact</a>, the League joined the Congress in the proposal for greater self-government that was campaigned for by Tilak and his supporters; in return, the Congress accepted separate electorates for Muslims in the provincial legislatures as well as the Imperial Legislative Council. In 1916, the Muslim League had anywhere between 500 and 800 members and did not yet have its wider following among Indian Muslims of later years; in the League itself, the pact did not have unanimous backing, having largely been negotiated by a group of "Young Party" Muslims from the <a href="/wiki/United_Provinces_of_Agra_and_Oudh" title="United Provinces of Agra and Oudh">United Provinces</a> (UP), most prominently, two brothers <a href="/wiki/Maulana_Mohammad_Ali" class="mw-redirect" title="Maulana Mohammad Ali">Mohammad</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maulana_Shaukat_Ali" class="mw-redirect" title="Maulana Shaukat Ali">Shaukat Ali</a>, who had embraced the Pan-Islamic cause;<sup id="cite_ref-brown-p200-201_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brown-p200-201-24">[24]</a></sup> however, it did have the support of a young lawyer from Bombay, <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali_Jinnah" title="Muhammad Ali Jinnah">Muhammad Ali Jinnah</a>, who was later to rise to leadership roles in both the League and the Indian independence movement. In later years, as the full ramifications of the pact unfolded, it was seen as benefiting the Muslim minority <i>élites</i> of provinces like UP and Bihar more than the Muslim majorities of Punjab and Bengal; nonetheless, at the time, the "Lucknow Pact", was an important milestone in nationalistic agitation and was seen so by the British.<sup id="cite_ref-brown-p200-201_24-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brown-p200-201-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Montague.E2.80.93Chelmsford_Reforms:_1919">Montague–Chelmsford Reforms: 1919</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Partition_of_India&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Montague–Chelmsford Reforms: 1919">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Montague and Chelmsford presented their report in July 1918 after a long fact-finding trip through India the previous winter.<sup id="cite_ref-brown-p205-207_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brown-p205-207-25">[25]</a></sup> After more discussion by the government and parliament in Britain, and another tour by the Franchise and Functions Committee for the purpose of identifying who among the Indian population could vote in future elections, the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_India_Act_of_1919" class="mw-redirect" title="Government of India Act of 1919">Government of India Act of 1919</a> (also known as the <a href="/wiki/Montagu%E2%80%93Chelmsford_Reforms" title="Montagu–Chelmsford Reforms">Montagu–Chelmsford Reforms</a>) was passed in December 1919.<sup id="cite_ref-brown-p205-207_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brown-p205-207-25">[25]</a></sup> The new Act enlarged both the provincial and <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Legislative_Council" title="Imperial Legislative Council">Imperial</a> legislative councils and repealed the Government of India's recourse to the "official majority" in unfavorable votes.<sup id="cite_ref-brown-p205-207_25-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brown-p205-207-25">[25]</a></sup> Although departments like defence, foreign affairs, criminal law, communications, and income-tax were retained by the <a href="/wiki/Governor-General_of_India" title="Governor-General of India">Viceroy</a> and the central government in New Delhi, other departments like public health, education, land-revenue, local self-government were transferred to the provinces.<sup id="cite_ref-brown-p205-207_25-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brown-p205-207-25">[25]</a></sup> The provinces themselves were now to be administered under a new <a href="/wiki/Diarchy" title="Diarchy">dyarchical</a> system, whereby some areas like education, agriculture, infrastructure development, and local self-government became the preserve of Indian ministers and legislatures, and ultimately the Indian electorates, while others like irrigation, land-revenue, police, prisons, and control of media remained within the purview of the British governor and his executive council.<sup id="cite_ref-brown-p205-207_25-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brown-p205-207-25">[25]</a></sup> The new Act also made it easier for Indians to be admitted into the civil service and the army officer corps.</p>
<p>A greater number of Indians were now enfranchised, although, for voting at the national level, they constituted only 10% of the total adult male population, many of whom were still illiterate.<sup id="cite_ref-brown-p205-207_25-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brown-p205-207-25">[25]</a></sup> In the provincial legislatures, the British continued to exercise some control by setting aside seats for special interests they considered cooperative or useful. In particular, rural candidates, generally sympathetic to British rule and less confrontational, were assigned more seats than their urban counterparts.<sup id="cite_ref-brown-p205-207_25-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brown-p205-207-25">[25]</a></sup> Seats were also reserved for non-Brahmins, landowners, businessmen, and college graduates. The principle of "communal representation," an integral part of the <a href="/wiki/Minto-Morley_Reforms" class="mw-redirect" title="Minto-Morley Reforms">Minto-Morley Reforms</a>, and more recently of the Congress-Muslim League Lucknow Pact, was reaffirmed, with seats being reserved for <a href="/wiki/Muslims" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslims">Muslims</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sikhs" class="mw-redirect" title="Sikhs">Sikhs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_India" title="Christianity in India">Indian Christians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Indians" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Indians">Anglo-Indians</a>, and domiciled Europeans, in both provincial and Imperial legislative councils.<sup id="cite_ref-brown-p205-207_25-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brown-p205-207-25">[25]</a></sup> The Montague-Chelmsford reforms offered Indians the most significant opportunity yet for exercising legislative power, especially at the provincial level; however, that opportunity was also restricted by the still limited number of eligible voters, by the small budgets available to provincial legislatures, and by the presence of rural and special interest seats that were seen as instruments of British control.<sup id="cite_ref-brown-p205-207_25-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brown-p205-207-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Two_nation_theory">Two nation theory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Partition_of_India&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Two nation theory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Two-nation_theory" title="Two-nation theory">Two-nation theory</a></div>
<p>The two-nation is the <a href="/wiki/Ideology" title="Ideology">ideology</a> that the primary identity and unifying denominator of <a href="/wiki/Muslims" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslims">Muslims</a> in the <a href="/wiki/South_Asian_subcontinent" class="mw-redirect" title="South Asian subcontinent">South Asian subcontinent</a> is their religion, rather than their <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">language</a> or <a href="/wiki/Ethnicity" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethnicity">ethnicity</a>, and therefore Indian <a href="/wiki/Hindus" class="mw-redirect" title="Hindus">Hindus</a> and Muslims are two distinct nations, regardless of ethnic or other commonalities.<sup id="cite_ref-winks2001_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-winks2001-26">[26]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-khan1940_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-khan1940-27">[27]</a></sup> The two-nation theory was a founding principle of the <a href="/wiki/Pakistan_Movement" title="Pakistan Movement">Pakistan Movement</a> (i.e. the ideology of <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> as a Muslim <a href="/wiki/Nation-state" class="mw-redirect" title="Nation-state">nation-state</a> in South Asia), and the partition of India in 1947.<sup id="cite_ref-Two-Nation_Theory_Exists_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Two-Nation_Theory_Exists-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>The ideology that religion is the determining factor in defining the nationality of Indian Muslims was undertaken by <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali_Jinnah" title="Muhammad Ali Jinnah">Muhammad Ali Jinnah</a>, who termed it as the awakening of Muslims for the creation of Pakistan.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> It is also a source of inspiration to several <a href="/wiki/Hindu_nationalism" title="Hindu nationalism">Hindu nationalist</a> organizations, with causes as varied as the redefinition of Indian Muslims as non-Indian foreigners and second-class citizens in India, the <a href="/wiki/Persecution_of_Muslims_in_India" class="mw-redirect" title="Persecution of Muslims in India">expulsion of all Muslims</a> from <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, establishment of a legally Hindu state in India, prohibition of conversions to <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, and the promotion of <a href="/wiki/Shuddhi" title="Shuddhi">conversions or reconversions</a> of Indian Muslims to Hinduism.<sup id="cite_ref-epw1979r_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epw1979r-30">[30]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sankhdher1991_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sankhdher1991-31">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-savarkar1989_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-savarkar1989-32">[32]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-chakravarty1990_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chakravarty1990-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are varying interpretations of the two-nation theory, based on whether the two postulated nationalities can coexist in one territory or not, with radically different implications. One interpretation argued for sovereign autonomy, including the right to secede, for Muslim-majority areas of the Indian subcontinent, but without any transfer of populations (i.e. Hindus and Muslims would continue to live together). A different interpretation contends that Hindus and Muslims constitute "two distinct, and frequently antagonistic ways of life, and that therefore they cannot coexist in one nation."<sup id="cite_ref-caldarola1982_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-caldarola1982-34">[34]</a></sup> In this version, a transfer of populations (i.e. the total removal of Hindus from Muslim-majority areas and the total removal of Muslims from Hindu-majority areas) is a desirable step towards a complete separation of two incompatible nations that "cannot coexist in a harmonious relationship".<sup id="cite_ref-harman1977_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-harman1977-35">[35]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sankhdher1992_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sankhdher1992-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>Opposition to the theory has come from two sources. The first is the concept of a <a href="/wiki/Greater_India" title="Greater India">single Indian nation</a>, of which Hindus and Muslims are two intertwined communities.<sup id="cite_ref-zakaria2004_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zakaria2004-37">[37]</a></sup> This is a founding principle of the modern, officially secular, <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_India" class="mw-redirect" title="Republic of India">Republic of India</a>. Even after the formation of Pakistan, debates on whether Muslims and Hindus are distinct nationalities or not continued in that country as well.<sup id="cite_ref-gop1953_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gop1953-38">[38]</a></sup> The second source of opposition is the concept that while Indians are not one nation, neither are the Muslims or Hindus of the subcontinent, and it is instead the relatively homogeneous provincial units of the subcontinent which are true nations and deserving of sovereignty; this view has been presented by the Baloch,<sup id="cite_ref-janmahmad1989_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-janmahmad1989-39">[39]</a></sup> Sindhi,<sup id="cite_ref-cohen2004_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cohen2004-40">[40]</a></sup> and Pashtun<sup id="cite_ref-salim1991_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-salim1991-41">[41]</a></sup> sub-nationalities of Pakistan.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Muslim_homeland.2C_provincial_elections.2C_World_War_II.2C_Lahore_Resolution:_1930.E2.80.931945">Muslim homeland, provincial elections, World War II, Lahore Resolution: 1930–1945</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Partition_of_India&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Muslim homeland, provincial elections, World War II, Lahore Resolution: 1930–1945">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<p>Although <a href="/wiki/Choudhry_Rahmat_Ali" title="Choudhry Rahmat Ali">Choudhry Rahmat Ali</a> had in 1933 produced a pamphlet, <i><a href="/wiki/Pakistan_Declaration" title="Pakistan Declaration">Now or never</a></i>, in which the term "<a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>", "the land of the pure", comprising the <a href="/wiki/Punjab_Pakistan" class="mw-redirect" title="Punjab Pakistan">Punjab</a>, <a href="/wiki/North-West_Frontier_Province_(1901%E2%80%931955)" class="mw-redirect" title="North-West Frontier Province (1901–1955)">North West Frontier Province (Afghania)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kashmir" title="Kashmir">Kashmir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sind_Province_(1936%E2%80%9355)" title="Sind Province (1936–55)">Sindh</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Balochistan_(Chief_Commissioner%27s_Province)" class="mw-redirect" title="Balochistan (Chief Commissioner's Province)">Balochistan</a>, was coined for the first time, the pamphlet did not attract political attention.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200931_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200931-42">[42]</a></sup> A little later, a Muslim delegation to the Parliamentary Committee on Indian Constitutional Reforms gave short shrift to the Pakistan idea, calling it "chimerical and impracticable".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200931_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200931-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>Two years later, the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_India_Act_1935" class="mw-redirect" title="Government of India Act 1935">Government of India Act 1935</a> introduced provincial autonomy, increasing the number of voters in India to 35 million.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200932_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200932-43">[43]</a></sup> More significantly, law and order issues were for the first time devolved from British authority to provincial governments headed by Indians.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200932_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200932-43">[43]</a></sup> This increased Muslim anxieties about eventual Hindu domination.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200932_43-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200932-43">[43]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Indian_provincial_elections,_1937" title="Indian provincial elections, 1937">Indian provincial elections, 1937</a>, the Muslim League turned out its best performance in Muslim-minority provinces such as the <a href="/wiki/United_Provinces_of_Agra_and_Oudh" title="United Provinces of Agra and Oudh">United Provinces</a>, where it won 29 of the 64 reserved Muslim seats.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200932_43-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200932-43">[43]</a></sup> However, in the Muslim-majority regions of the Punjab and Bengal regional parties outperformed the League.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200932_43-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200932-43">[43]</a></sup> In the Punjab, the <a href="/wiki/Unionist_Party_(Punjab)" title="Unionist Party (Punjab)">Unionist Part</a> of <a href="/wiki/Sikandar_Hayat_Khan_(Punjabi_politician)" title="Sikandar Hayat Khan (Punjabi politician)">Sikandar Hayat Khan</a>, won the elections and formed a government, with the support of the Indian National Congress and the <a href="/wiki/Shiromani_Akali_Dal" title="Shiromani Akali Dal">Shiromani Akali Dal</a>, which lasted five years.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200932_43-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200932-43">[43]</a></sup> In Bengal, the League had to share power in a coalition headed by <a href="/wiki/A._K._Fazlul_Huq" title="A. K. Fazlul Huq">A. K. Fazlul Huq</a>, the leader of the <a href="/wiki/Krishak_Praja_Party" class="mw-redirect" title="Krishak Praja Party">Krishak Praja Party</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200932_43-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200932-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Congress, on the other hand, with 716 wins in the total of 1585 provincial assemblies seats, was able to form governments in 7 out of the 11 provinces of <a href="/wiki/British_India" class="mw-redirect" title="British India">British India</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200932_43-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200932-43">[43]</a></sup> In its manifesto the Congress maintained that religious issues were of lesser importance to the masses than economic and social issues, however, the election revealed that the Congress had contested just 58 out of the total 482 Muslim seats, and of these, it won in only 26.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200932_43-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200932-43">[43]</a></sup> In UP, where the Congress won, it offered to share power with the League on condition that the League stop functioning as a representative only of Muslims, which the League refused.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200932_43-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200932-43">[43]</a></sup> This proved to be a mistake as it alienated the Congress further from the Muslim masses. In addition, the new UP provincial administration promulgated cow protection and the use of Hindi.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200932_43-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200932-43">[43]</a></sup> The Muslim elite in UP was further alienated, when they saw chaotic scenes of the new Congress Raj, in which rural people who sometimes turned up in large numbers in Government buildings, were indistinguishable from the administrators and the law enforcement personnel.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200932.E2.80.9333_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200932.E2.80.9333-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Muslim League conducted its own investigation into the conditions of Muslims under Congress-governed provinces.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200933_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200933-45">[45]</a></sup> The findings of such investigations increased fear among the Muslim masses of future Hindu domination.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200933_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200933-45">[45]</a></sup> The view that Muslims would be unfairly treated in an independent India dominated by the Congress was now a part of the public discourse of Muslims.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200933_45-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200933-45">[45]</a></sup> With the outbreak of World War II in 1939, the viceroy, <a href="/wiki/Victor_Hope,_2nd_Marquess_of_Linlithgow" title="Victor Hope, 2nd Marquess of Linlithgow">Lord Linlithgow</a>, declared war on India's behalf without consulting Indian leaders, leading the Congress provincial ministries to resign in protest.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200933_45-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200933-45">[45]</a></sup> The Muslim League, which functioned under state patronage,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200934_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200934-46">[46]</a></sup> in contrast, organized "Deliverance Day," celebrations (from Congress dominance) and supported Britain in the war effort.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200933_45-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200933-45">[45]</a></sup> When Linlithgow, met with nationalist leaders, he gave the same status to Jinnah as he did to Gandhi, and a month later described the Congress as a "Hindu organization."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200934_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200934-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>In March 1940, in the League's annual three-day session in <a href="/wiki/Lahore" title="Lahore">Lahore</a>, Jinnah gave a two-hour speech in English, in which were laid out the arguments of the <a href="/wiki/Two-nation_theory" title="Two-nation theory">Two-nation theory</a>, stating, in the words of historians Talbot and Singh, that "Muslims and Hindus ... were irreconcilably opposed monolithic religious communities and as such no settlement could be imposed that did not satisfy the aspirations of the former."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200933_45-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200933-45">[45]</a></sup> On the last day of its session, the League passed, what came to be known as the <a href="/wiki/Lahore_Resolution" title="Lahore Resolution">Lahore Resolution</a>, sometimes also "Pakistan Resolution",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200933_45-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200933-45">[45]</a></sup> demanding that "the areas in which the Muslims are numerically in majority as in the North-Western and Eastern zones of India should be grouped to constitute independent states in which the constituent units shall be autonomous and sovereign." Though it had been founded more than three decades earlier, the League would gather support among South Asian Muslims only during the Second World War.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKhan200718_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKhan200718-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>In March 1942, with the Japanese fast moving up the <a href="/wiki/Malayan_Peninsula" class="mw-redirect" title="Malayan Peninsula">Malayan Peninsula</a> after the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Singapore" class="mw-redirect" title="Fall of Singapore">Fall of Singapore</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200934_46-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200934-46">[46]</a></sup> and with the Americans supporting independence for India,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200934.E2.80.9335_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200934.E2.80.9335-48">[48]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a>, the wartime Prime Minister of Britain, sent Sir <a href="/wiki/Stafford_Cripps" title="Stafford Cripps">Stafford Cripps</a>, the leader of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="House of Commons of the United Kingdom">House of Commons</a>, with an offer of <a href="/wiki/Dominion" title="Dominion">dominion</a> status to India at the end of the war in return for the Congress's support for the war effort.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200935_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200935-49">[49]</a></sup> Not wishing to lose the support of the allies they had already secured—the Muslim League, Unionists of the Punjab, and the Princes—the Cripps offer included a clause stating that no part of the British Indian Empire would be forced to join the post-war Dominion. As a result of the proviso, the proposals were rejected by the Congress, which, since its founding as a polite group of lawyers in 1885,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKhan200718_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKhan200718-47">[47]</a></sup> saw itself as the representative of all Indians of all faiths.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200935_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200935-49">[49]</a></sup> After the arrival in 1920 of Gandhi, the preeminent strategist of Indian nationalism,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESteinArnold2010289_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESteinArnold2010289-50">[50]</a></sup> the Congress had been transformed into a mass nationalist movement of millions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKhan200718_47-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKhan200718-47">[47]</a></sup> In August 1942, the Congress launched the <a href="/wiki/Quit_India_Resolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Quit India Resolution">Quit India Resolution</a> which asked for drastic constitutional changes, which the British saw as the most serious threat to their rule since the <a href="/wiki/Indian_rebellion_of_1857" class="mw-redirect" title="Indian rebellion of 1857">Indian rebellion of 1857</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200935_49-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200935-49">[49]</a></sup> With their resources and attention already spread thin by a global war, the nervous British immediately jailed the Congress leaders and kept them in jail until August 1945,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMetcalfMetcalf2006209_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMetcalfMetcalf2006209-51">[51]</a></sup> whereas the Muslim League was now free for the next three years to spread its message.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200934_46-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200934-46">[46]</a></sup> Consequently, the Muslim League's ranks surged during the war, with Jinnah himself admitting, "The war which nobody welcomed proved to be a blessing in disguise."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKhan200743_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKhan200743-52">[52]</a></sup> Although there were other important national Muslim politicians such as Congress leader <a href="/wiki/Abul_Kalam_Azad" title="Abul Kalam Azad">Abul Kalam Azad</a>, and influential regional Muslim politicians such as <a href="/wiki/A._K._Fazlul_Huq" title="A. K. Fazlul Huq">A. K. Fazlul Huq</a> of the leftist <a href="/wiki/Krishak_Praja_Party" class="mw-redirect" title="Krishak Praja Party">Krishak Praja Party</a> in Bengal, <a href="/wiki/Sikander_Hyat_Khan" class="mw-redirect" title="Sikander Hyat Khan">Sikander Hyat Khan</a> of the landlord-dominated <a href="/wiki/Unionist_Muslim_League" class="mw-redirect" title="Unionist Muslim League">Punjab Unionist Party</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Abdul_Ghaffar_Khan" class="mw-redirect" title="Abdul Ghaffar Khan">Abd al-Ghaffar Khan</a> of the pro-Congress <a href="/wiki/Khudai_Khidmatgar" title="Khudai Khidmatgar">Khudai Khidmatgar</a> (popularly, "red shirts") in the <a href="/wiki/North-West_Frontier_Province_(1901%E2%80%931955)" class="mw-redirect" title="North-West Frontier Province (1901–1955)">North West Frontier Province</a>, the British were to increasingly see the League as the main representative of Muslim India.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1946_Election.2C_Cabinet_Mission.2C_Direct_Action_Day.2C_Plan_for_Partition.2C_Independence_1946.E2.80.931947">1946 Election, Cabinet Mission, Direct Action Day, Plan for Partition, Independence 1946–1947</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Partition_of_India&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: 1946 Election, Cabinet Mission, Direct Action Day, Plan for Partition, Independence 1946–1947">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
