<td>1,500,000</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The population in the Tharparkar district in the Sind province of West Pakistan was 80% Hindu and 20% Muslim at the time of independence in 1947. During the Indo-Pak wars of 1965 and 1971, the Hindu upper castes and their retainers fled to India. This led to a massive demographic shift in the district. According to the 1998 census of Pakistan, Muslims made up 64.42% of the population and Hindus 35.58% of the population of Tharparkar.</p>
<p>Due to religious persecution in Pakistan, Hindus continue to flee to India. Most of them tend to settle in the state of Rajasthan in India.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> According to the Human Rights Commission of Pakistan data, just around 1,000 Hindu families fled to India in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> In May 2014, a member of the ruling Pakistan Muslim League-Nawaz (PML-N), Dr Ramesh Kumar Vankwani, revealed in the National Assembly of Pakistan that around 5,000 Hindus are migrating from Pakistan to India every year.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Perspectives">Perspectives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Partition_of_India&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Perspectives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
<div id="mwe_player_0" class="PopUpMediaTransform" style="width:220px;" videopayload="&lt;div class=&quot;mediaContainer&quot; style=&quot;width:320px&quot;&gt;&lt;video id=&quot;mwe_player_1&quot; poster=&quot;//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Refugees_on_train_roof_during_Partition.ogg/320px--Refugees_on_train_roof_during_Partition.ogg.jpg&quot; controls=&quot;&quot; preload=&quot;none&quot; autoplay=&quot;&quot; style=&quot;width:320px;height:240px&quot; class=&quot;kskin&quot; data-durationhint=&quot;5&quot; data-startoffset=&quot;0&quot; data-mwtitle=&quot;Refugees_on_train_roof_during_Partition.ogg&quot; data-mwprovider=&quot;wikimediacommons&quot;&gt;&lt;source src=&quot;//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2f/Refugees_on_train_roof_during_Partition.ogg&quot; type=&quot;video/ogg; codecs=&amp;quot;theora&amp;quot;&quot; data-title=&quot;Original Ogg file, 320 × 240 (518 kbps)&quot; data-shorttitle=&quot;Ogg source&quot; data-width=&quot;320&quot; data-height=&quot;240&quot; data-bandwidth=&quot;518002&quot; data-framerate=&quot;10&quot;/&gt;&lt;source src=&quot;//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/2/2f/Refugees_on_train_roof_during_Partition.ogg/Refugees_on_train_roof_during_Partition.ogg.160p.ogv&quot; type=&quot;video/ogg; codecs=&amp;quot;theora, vorbis&amp;quot;&quot; data-title=&quot;Low bandwidth Ogg video (160P)&quot; data-shorttitle=&quot;Ogg 160P&quot; data-transcodekey=&quot;160p.ogv&quot; data-width=&quot;214&quot; data-height=&quot;160&quot; data-bandwidth=&quot;189472&quot; data-framerate=&quot;15&quot;/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;"><img alt="File:Refugees on train roof during Partition.ogg" style="width:220px;height:165px" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Refugees_on_train_roof_during_Partition.ogg/220px--Refugees_on_train_roof_during_Partition.ogg.jpg" /><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2f/Refugees_on_train_roof_during_Partition.ogg" title="Play media" target="new"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Refugees_on_train_roof_during_Partition.ogg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Refugees on train roof during Partition</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Partition was a highly controversial arrangement, and remains a cause of much tension on the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a> today. The British <a href="/wiki/Viceroy" title="Viceroy">Viceroy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Louis_Mountbatten,_1st_Earl_Mountbatten_of_Burma" title="Louis Mountbatten, 1st Earl Mountbatten of Burma">Lord Mountbatten of Burma</a> has not only been accused of rushing the process through, but also is alleged to have influenced the <a href="/wiki/Radcliffe_Line" title="Radcliffe Line">Radcliffe Line</a> in India's favour.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> The commission took longer to decide on a final boundary than on the partition itself. Thus the two nations were granted their independence even before there was a defined boundary between them.</p>
<p>Some critics allege that British haste led to increased cruelties during the Partition.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> Because independence was declared <i>prior</i> to the actual Partition, it was up to the new governments of India and Pakistan to keep public order. No large population movements were contemplated; the plan called for safeguards for minorities on both sides of the new border. It was a task at which both states failed. There was a complete breakdown of law and order; many died in riots, massacre, or just from the hardships of their flight to safety. What ensued was one of the largest population movements in recorded history. According to <a href="/wiki/Richard_Symonds_(academic)" title="Richard Symonds (academic)">Richard Symonds</a>, at the lowest estimate, half a million people perished and twelve million became homeless.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, many argue that the British were forced to expedite the Partition by events on the ground.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrence_p_72_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrence_p_72-110">[110]</a></sup> Once in office, Mountbatten quickly became aware if Britain were to avoid involvement in a civil war, which seemed increasingly likely, there was no alternative to partition and a hasty exit from India.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrence_p_72_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrence_p_72-110">[110]</a></sup> Law and order had broken down many times before Partition, with much bloodshed on both sides. A massive civil war was looming by the time Mountbatten became Viceroy. After the Second World War, Britain had limited resources,<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> perhaps insufficient to the task of keeping order. Another viewpoint is that while Mountbatten may have been too hasty he had no real options left and achieved the best he could under difficult circumstances.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> The historian Lawrence James concurs that in 1947 Mountbatten was left with no option but to cut and run. The alternative seemed to be involvement in a potentially bloody civil war from which it would be difficult to get out.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup></p>
<p>Conservative elements in England consider the partition of India to be the moment that the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> ceased to be a world power, following <a href="/wiki/George_Curzon,_1st_Marquess_Curzon_of_Kedleston" title="George Curzon, 1st Marquess Curzon of Kedleston">Curzon</a>'s dictum: "the loss of India would mean that Britain drop straight away to a third rate power."<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup></p>
<p>A cross-border student initiative, <i>The History Project</i>, was launched in 2014 to explore the differences in perception of the events during the British era which led to the partition. The project resulted in a book that explains both interpretations of the shared history in Pakistan and India.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Artistic_depictions_of_the_Partition">Artistic depictions of the Partition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Partition_of_India&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Artistic depictions of the Partition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Artistic_depictions_of_the_partition_of_India" title="Artistic depictions of the partition of India">Artistic depictions of the partition of India</a></div>
<p>The partition of India and the associated bloody riots inspired many in <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> and Pakistan to create literary/cinematic depictions of this event.<sup id="cite_ref-Cleary2002_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cleary2002-117">[117]</a></sup> While some creations depicted the massacres during the refugee migration, others concentrated on the aftermath of the partition in terms of difficulties faced by the refugees in both side of the border. Even now, more than 60 years after the partition, works of fiction and films are made that relate to the events of partition. The early members of the Progressive Artist's Group of Bombay cite "The Partition" of India and Pakistan as a key reason for its founding in December 1947. They included FN Souza, MF Husain, SH Raza, SK Bakre, HA Gade and KH Ara, who went on to become some of the most important and influential Indian artists of the 20th Century.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup></p>
<p>Literature describing the human cost of independence and partition comprises Bal K. Gupta's memoirs <i>Forgotten Atrocities</i> (2012), <a href="/wiki/Khushwant_Singh" title="Khushwant Singh">Khushwant Singh</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Train_to_Pakistan" title="Train to Pakistan">Train to Pakistan</a></i> (1956), several short stories such as <i><a href="/wiki/Toba_Tek_Singh_(short_story)" title="Toba Tek Singh (short story)">Toba Tek Singh</a></i> (1955) by <a href="/wiki/Saadat_Hassan_Manto" class="mw-redirect" title="Saadat Hassan Manto">Saadat Hassan Manto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Urdu" title="Urdu">Urdu</a> poems such as <i>Subh-e-Azadi</i> (Freedom's Dawn, 1947) by <a href="/wiki/Faiz_Ahmad_Faiz" title="Faiz Ahmad Faiz">Faiz Ahmad Faiz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bhisham_Sahni" title="Bhisham Sahni">Bhisham Sahni</a>'s <i>Tamas</i> (1974), <a href="/wiki/Manohar_Malgonkar" title="Manohar Malgonkar">Manohar Malgonkar</a>'s <i>A Bend in the Ganges</i> (1965), and <a href="/wiki/Bapsi_Sidhwa" title="Bapsi Sidhwa">Bapsi Sidhwa</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Cracking_India" title="Cracking India">Ice-Candy Man</a></i> (1988), among others.<sup id="cite_ref-Natarajan1996_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Natarajan1996-119">[119]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Roy2011_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roy2011-120">[120]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Salman_Rushdie" title="Salman Rushdie">Salman Rushdie</a>'s novel <i><a href="/wiki/Midnight%27s_Children" title="Midnight's Children">Midnight's Children</a></i> (1980), which won the <a href="/wiki/Booker_Prize" class="mw-redirect" title="Booker Prize">Booker Prize</a> and the <a href="/wiki/The_Best_of_the_Booker" title="The Best of the Booker">Booker of Bookers</a>, wove its narrative based on the children born with magical abilities on midnight of 14 August 1947.<sup id="cite_ref-Roy2011_120-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roy2011-120">[120]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Freedom_at_Midnight" title="Freedom at Midnight">Freedom at Midnight</a></i> (1975) is a non-fiction work by <a href="/wiki/Larry_Collins_(writer)" title="Larry Collins (writer)">Larry Collins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dominique_Lapierre" title="Dominique Lapierre">Dominique Lapierre</a> that chronicled the events surrounding the first Independence Day celebrations in 1947.</p>
<p>There is a paucity of films related to the independence and partition.<sup id="cite_ref-Bhatia2008_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bhatia2008-121">[121]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hindi_guide_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hindi_guide-122">[122]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sarkar2009_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sarkar2009-123">[123]</a></sup> Early films relating to the circumstances of the independence, partition and the aftermath include <a href="/wiki/Nemai_Ghosh_(director)" title="Nemai Ghosh (director)">Nemai Ghosh</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Chinnamul" title="Chinnamul">Chinnamul</a></i> (Bengali) (1950),<sup id="cite_ref-Bhatia2008_121-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bhatia2008-121">[121]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Dharmputra" title="Dharmputra">Dharmputra</a></i> (1961)<sup id="cite_ref-vishwanath_2009_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vishwanath_2009-124">[124]</a></sup> Lahore (1948), Chhalia (1956), Nastik (1953). <a href="/wiki/Ritwik_Ghatak" title="Ritwik Ghatak">Ritwik Ghatak</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Meghe_Dhaka_Tara" title="Meghe Dhaka Tara">Meghe Dhaka Tara</a></i> (Bengali) (1960), <a href="/wiki/George_Cukor" title="George Cukor">George Cukor</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Bhowani_Junction_(film)" title="Bhowani Junction (film)">Bhowani Junction</a></i> (1956), <i><a href="/wiki/Komal_Gandhar" title="Komal Gandhar">Komal Gandhar</a></i> (Bengali) (1961), <i><a href="/wiki/Subarnarekha_(1962_film)" class="mw-redirect" title="Subarnarekha (1962 film)">Subarnarekha</a></i> (Bengali) (1962);<sup id="cite_ref-Bhatia2008_121-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bhatia2008-121">[121]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ritwik_journal_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ritwik_journal-125">[125]</a></sup> later films include <i><a href="/wiki/Garm_Hava" title="Garm Hava">Garm Hava</a></i> (1973) and <i><a href="/wiki/Tamas_(film)" title="Tamas (film)">Tamas</a></i> (1987).<sup id="cite_ref-vishwanath_2009_124-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vishwanath_2009-124">[124]</a></sup> From the late 1990s onwards, more films on this theme were made, including several mainstream ones, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Earth_(1998_film)" title="Earth (1998 film)">Earth</a></i> (1998), <i><a href="/wiki/Train_to_Pakistan_(film)" title="Train to Pakistan (film)">Train to Pakistan</a></i> (1998) (based on the aforementined book), <i><a href="/wiki/Hey_Ram" title="Hey Ram">Hey Ram</a></i> (2000), <i><a href="/wiki/Gadar:_Ek_Prem_Katha" title="Gadar: Ek Prem Katha">Gadar: Ek Prem Katha</a></i> (2001), <i><a href="/wiki/Khamosh_Pani" title="Khamosh Pani">Khamosh Pani</a></i> (2003), <i><a href="/wiki/Pinjar_(film)" title="Pinjar (film)">Pinjar</a></i> (2003), <i><a href="/wiki/Partition_(2007_film)" title="Partition (2007 film)">Partition</a></i> (2007) and <i><a href="/wiki/Madrasapattinam" title="Madrasapattinam">Madrasapattinam</a></i> (2010).<sup id="cite_ref-vishwanath_2009_124-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vishwanath_2009-124">[124]</a></sup> The biographical films <i><a href="/wiki/Gandhi_(film)" title="Gandhi (film)">Gandhi</a></i> (1982), <i><a href="/wiki/Jinnah_(film)" title="Jinnah (film)">Jinnah</a></i> (1998) and <i><a href="/wiki/Sardar_(1993_film)" title="Sardar (1993 film)">Sardar</a></i> (1993) also feature independence and partition as significant events in their screenplay. A Pakistani drama <i><a href="/wiki/Dastaan_(drama)" class="mw-redirect" title="Dastaan (drama)">Daastan</a></i>, based on the novel Bano, highlights the plight of Muslim girls who were abducted and raped during partition.</p>
<p>The novel <i>Lost Generations</i> (2013) by Manjit Sachdeva describes the March 1947 massacre in rural areas of Rawalpindi by the Muslim League, followed by massacres on both sides of the new border in August 1947 seen through the eyes of an escaping Sikh family, their settlement and partial rehabilitation in Delhi, and ending in ruin (including death), for the second time in 1984, at the hands of mobs after a Sikh assassinated the prime minister.</p>
<p>The 2013 Google India advertisement <i><a href="/wiki/Reunion_(advertisement)" title="Reunion (advertisement)">Reunion</a></i> (about the Partition of India) has had a strong impact in India and Pakistan, leading to hope for the easing of travel restrictions between the two countries.<sup id="cite_ref-expresstrib_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-expresstrib-126">[126]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-pti_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pti-127">[127]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-tears_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tears-128">[128]</a></sup> It <a href="/wiki/Viral_video" title="Viral video">went viral</a><sup id="cite_ref-ibt_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ibt-129">[129]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-tia_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tia-130">[130]</a></sup> and was viewed more than 1.6 million times before officially debuting on television on 15 November 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-apjohnson_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-apjohnson-131">[131]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Partition_of_India&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<li id="cite_note-khan1940-27"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-khan1940_27-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite id="CITEREFLiaquat_Ali_Khan1940" class="citation">Liaquat Ali Khan (1940), <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=swIYjzJOx5wC"><i>Pakistan: The Heart of Asia</i></a>, Thacker &amp; Co. Ltd., <q>... There is much in the Musalmans which, if they wish, can roll them into a nation. But isn't there enough that is common to both Hindus and Muslims, which if developed, is capable of molding them into one people? Nobody can deny that there are many modes, manners, rites and customs which are common to both. Nobody can deny that there are rites, customs and usages based on religion which do divide Hindus and Muslmans. The question is, which of these should be emphasized ...</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3APartition+of+India&amp;rft.au=Liaquat+Ali+Khan&amp;rft.btitle=Pakistan%3A+The+Heart+of+Asia&amp;rft.date=1940&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DswIYjzJOx5wC&amp;rft.pub=Thacker+%26+Co.+Ltd.&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>

Archived from <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.pakistantimes.net/2007/04/03/oped2.htm">the original</a> on 11 November 2007.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3APartition+of+India&amp;rft.btitle=Two-Nation+Theory+Exists&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.pakistantimes.net%2F2007%2F04%2F03%2Foped2.htm&amp;rft.pub=Pakistan+Times&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-29"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-29">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Jinnah: "Islam and Hinduism are not religions in the strict sense of the word, but in fact different and distinct social orders, and it is only a dream that the Hindus and Muslims can ever evolve a common nationality.... To yoke together two such nations under a single state ... must lead to a growing discontent and final destruction of any fabric that may be so built up for the government of such a state.<a rel="nofollow" class="external free" href="https://www.theatlantic.com/past/docs/issues/88aug/obrien.htm">https://www.theatlantic.com/past/docs/issues/88aug/obrien.htm</a></span></li>

<li id="cite_note-:2-57"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-:2_57-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-:2_57-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Mohiuddin, Yasmin Niaz (2007). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com.au/books?id=OTMy0B9OZjAC&amp;pg=PA68&amp;dq=support+for+pakistan+movement&amp;hl=en&amp;sa=X&amp;ved=0ahUKEwjr6pT2srPPAhXNNpQKHcpeB0gQ6AEIOTAF#v=onepage&amp;q=support%20for%20pakistan%20movement&amp;f=false"><i>Pakistan: A Global Studies Handbook</i></a>. ABC-CLIO. p.&#160;70. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9781851098019" title="Special:BookSources/9781851098019">9781851098019</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3APartition+of+India&amp;rft.aufirst=Yasmin+Niaz&amp;rft.aulast=Mohiuddin&amp;rft.btitle=Pakistan%3A+A+Global+Studies+Handbook&amp;rft.date=2007&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com.au%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DOTMy0B9OZjAC%26pg%3DPA68%26dq%3Dsupport%2Bfor%2Bpakistan%2Bmovement%26hl%3Den%26sa%3DX%26ved%3D0ahUKEwjr6pT2srPPAhXNNpQKHcpeB0gQ6AEIOTAF%23v%3Donepage%26q%3Dsupport%2520for%2520pakistan%2520movement%26f%3Dfalse&amp;rft.isbn=9781851098019&amp;rft.pages=70&amp;rft.pub=ABC-CLIO&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-58"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-58">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Mohiuddin, Yasmin Niaz (2007). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com.au/books?id=OTMy0B9OZjAC&amp;pg=PA68&amp;dq=support+for+pakistan+movement&amp;hl=en&amp;sa=X&amp;ved=0ahUKEwjr6pT2srPPAhXNNpQKHcpeB0gQ6AEIOTAF#v=onepage&amp;q=support%20for%20pakistan%20movement&amp;f=false"><i>Pakistan: A Global Studies Handbook</i></a>. ABC-CLIO. p.&#160;71. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9781851098019" title="Special:BookSources/9781851098019">9781851098019</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3APartition+of+India&amp;rft.aufirst=Yasmin+Niaz&amp;rft.aulast=Mohiuddin&amp;rft.btitle=Pakistan%3A+A+Global+Studies+Handbook&amp;rft.date=2007&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com.au%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DOTMy0B9OZjAC%26pg%3DPA68%26dq%3Dsupport%2Bfor%2Bpakistan%2Bmovement%26hl%3Den%26sa%3DX%26ved%3D0ahUKEwjr6pT2srPPAhXNNpQKHcpeB0gQ6AEIOTAF%23v%3Donepage%26q%3Dsupport%2520for%2520pakistan%2520movement%26f%3Dfalse&amp;rft.isbn=9781851098019&amp;rft.pages=71&amp;rft.pub=ABC-CLIO&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEKhan200764.E2.80.9365-59"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKhan200764.E2.80.9365_59-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFKhan2007">Khan 2007</a>, pp.&#160;64–65.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200969-60"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200969_60-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFTalbotSingh2009">Talbot &amp; Singh 2009</a>, p.&#160;69: Quote: "Despite the Muslim League's denials, the outbreak was clearly linked with the celebration of Direction Action Day. Muslim processionists who had gone to the staging ground of the 150-feet-high <a href="/wiki/Ochterlony_Monument" class="mw-redirect" title="Ochterlony Monument">Ochterlony Monument</a> on the maidan to hear the Muslim League Prime Minister Suhrawardy, attacked Hindus on their way back. They were heard shouting slogans as 'Larke Lenge Pakistan' (We shall win Pakistan by force). Violence spread to North Calcutta when Muslim crowds tried to force Hindu shopkeepers to observe the day's strike (<i>hartal</i>) call. The circulation of pamphlets in advance of Direct Action Day made a clear connection with the use of violence and the demand for Pakistan."</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-61"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-61">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFTalbotSingh2009">Talbot &amp; Singh 2009</a>, p.&#160;67 Quote: "The signs of 'ethnic cleansing' are first evident in the Great Calcutta Killing of 16–19 August 1946."</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200968-62"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTETalbotSingh200968_62-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFTalbotSingh2009">Talbot &amp; Singh 2009</a>, p.&#160;68.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-63"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-63">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFTalbotSingh2009">Talbot &amp; Singh 2009</a>, p.&#160;67 Quote: "(Signs of 'ethnic cleansing') were also present in the wave of violence that rippled out from Calcutta to Bihar, where there were high Muslim casualty figures, and to Noakhali deep in the Ganges-Brahmaputra delta of Bengal. With respect to the Noakhali riots, one British officer spoke of a 'determined and organised' Muslim effort to drive out all the Hindus, who accounted for around a fifth of the total population. Similarly, the Punjab counterpart to this transition of violence were the Rawalpindi massacres of March 1947. The level of death and destruction in such West Punjab villages as Thoa Khalsa was such that it was impossible for communities to live together in its wake."</span></li>
